1400069289|4.0|This is easily one of the best business books of 2012. Part of its power is that it weaves the same basic principles through various fields of study (e.g., personal life, science, marketing, organizational behavior, etc.).<br /><br />As the author, Charles Duhigg, demonstrates in a fluid narrative, there is a growing body of literature on the science of habits. And while there is no magic pill, academic pedagogy, or high-priced consultant that can instantly rid us of harmful habits or help us create positive practices, a correct understanding of the principles involved sheds light on the tools we need to take control of aspects of our lives so engrained in habit we may sometimes (incorrectly) think they are beyond our ability to influence.<br /><br />In my opinion, this book has the potential for long-term staying power. As the literature continues to grow and offers more insights, I would even be interested in a second edition were Duhigg to pursue it. The science is so critical that this book could be published in several different versions with case studies focused on any sector of the market, various individual industries, and numerous time periods.<br /><br />The longer we live, the more habits we develop - for better or worse. Individuals who master the understanding and application of habits create a competitive advantage both for themselves in their respective fields and for the organizations they can influence.<br /><br />A mastery of habits can turn failure into success and success into legacy. Conversely, individuals and organizations who are slaves to their habits run a terrible risk of exacerbating their failures and falling tragically short of the potential legacies that could have been theirs had they the price of honest introspection and purposeful, educated, dedicated hard work.<br /><br />Duhigg's Power of Habit is an essential book for anyone desirous to modify habits in either their personal or professional lives. Again, there is no magic answer. Results require hard work. But the science Duhigg presents provides the basic foundation you will need to make changes in your life. If this book is used as an instruction manual instead of a casual read, it can be used to prove in your life the proverb, \\"Scientia potentia est\\" (i.e., knowledge is power).Results require hard work. B000IJ79V6|3.0|Superman 2 is a good film, and a good follow-up to the first Superman movie as it is.  However, it could have also been so much better.  If the studio had aloud Richard Donnor to finish the film and make it the way he originally intended then Superman 2, along with the first film, could have been one perfect epic instead of two separate films.  Instead Donnor was replaced with Richard Lester and the film is bogged down by this.  This review will be on the Lester version that was released in theaters.  I have not seen the Donnor cut yet, though I hear it is much better than this version.<br /><br />The story is that in stopping a terrorist plot and throwing a nuclear bomb into space, he releases General Zod and his two minions from the Phantom Zone.  Superman/Clark also reveals his identity to Lois.  Clark/Superman ultimately has to choose between the love of his life and saving the world.  He also has to fend off Zod and prevent him from taking over Earth.<br /><br />Once again Christopher Reeve does a phenomenal job as both Superman and Clark Kent.  Margot Kidder also returns as Lois, and does a great job once again.  The chemistry between them is one of the best aspects about the movie.  I like the moral dilemma Clark is put in.  He has to choose between his own happiness and being a symbol of hope for humanity.  The story and the moral is definitely one of the best things about the film.  Terrance Stamp also plays a great villain as Zod.<br /><br />However, like the first movie, I do not like how Lex Luthor is played up mostly for comedy in this movie.  I did find him funny and Gene Hackman does a good job playing him once again.  However, Lex should not just be funny; he should also be cold and calculating.  We do get that at points in the film, but not nearly enough.  His two henchmen from the first film are back in this as well.  They are funny, but I never liked Lex having bumbling henchmen.  It just makes him look more like a Saturday morning cartoon villain.  Plus, I do not buy them working for Lex again after what he did in the first movie.<br /><br />Another thing that I do not like is the more light-hearted tone in places, which I find extremely out of place.  In the production of the Superman 2 Donnor was let go and replaced with Lester to add a more light-heated tone to the film.  This is why you have goofy scenes like Zod and his henchmen's head on Mt. Rushmore.  Scenes like that seem out of place and clash with the more serious scenes that Donnor had shot.  Plus Lester added shots of Superman and the other Kryptonians displaying new abilities that come completely out of nowhere like the telekinesis beam or the magic kiss Superman uses at the end.<br /><br />I have to wonder what the studio heads were thinking when they hired Lester.  While the first superman movie was serious for the most part it was not that dark and had a lot of levity and comedic moments.  I do not see why they felt the need to make it more kid friendly.  Fortunately much of the film was already shot by Donnor before he was replaced with Lester, otherwise this would have been a vastly different movie.  Superman 3 is an example of what Superman 2 would have been like had Lester had complete control.<br /><br />Like the first movie I find the effects rather dated.  I know they were great at the time, but now I find them a bit cheap.  The effects did not ruin the movie for me though.  Many of the action scenes like the battle in Metropolis were entertaining.<br /><br />Overall if you like the first Donnor Superman movie, definitely check this out.  This film can get very silly but is still a good superhero movie.

Seemingly no details are spared in describing the setting and characters.<br /><br />Some people complain that it's hard to read because there are too many references to historical events and persons (mainly from French history, but also many others, such as the old great Greek philosophers) and that the average reader today would have little knowledge of.<br /><br />While this was certainly the case for me (I'm no French historian), I just used the context in which he was referring to these sources and if I couldn't understand how our why he was using them as examples, I would just look them up via wikipedia or Google.<br /><br />I must say that this is obviously not necessary, but I have learned a lot about French history so far because of this, and I went back to reading the passage I was up to in Les Mis with mixed feelings.  Sometimes I would spend 10 minutes to reference a name and afterwards I didn't feel it was worth my time because it didn't truly add to my life or the importance of the story.  Other times I would spend twice that time and feel it was completely worth it.<br /><br />Now, I only look things up when I feel truly compelled to, as if it were necessary to truly understanding what Hugo was trying to convey by referring to someone or some historical event.<br /><br />Overall, I've gotten used to his writing style and I love it.  The story is epic and I love how deeply we get to delve into each of the principal characters in the story.

In Strayed's case, her mother's death led to divorcing a husband she loved, teetering on the brink of heroin addiction, getting an abortion, and deciding to hike 1,100 miles on her own across the Pacific Crest Trail. Early on in this memoir we realize this is a healing journey filled with metaphors of life's unexpected twists and turns.<br /><br />As the author of a memoir about my husband's untimely death from colorectal cancer, just three weeks after his 47th birthday, I more than identified with Strayed's railing against the fates . . . how could her \\"good mother\\" die at 45, just weeks after her lung cancer diagnosis? Ours is not to question why, or in this case, maybe it means following the trail to learn how \\"mysterious\\" and \\"irrevocable\\" life is . .. to \\"let it be\\" as she finally concludes as she hikes through the wild. As the author sets out on her hike and her \\"search\\" for self and healing, we see a young woman's determination to test the fates and embark on a risky journey toward redemption. The compelling descriptions of California and the Pacific Northwest along the PCT are a hiker's delight, but by the end of this 316-page memoir, we have read one too many false scares ... bears, moose, rattlesnakes; not enough money waiting at the next post office along the trail where Strayed has forwarded her meager funds, leaving her thirsting for an ice cold Snapple lemonade but down to her last two cents. The book is rife with metaphor, the trail, the \\"pilgrims\\" she meets along her hike, most of whom are arrestingly portrayed, her guide book, The Pacific Crest Trail, Vols. 1 and 2 which is her bible.<br /><br />Maybe it is just me, but whenever a book is selected for Oprah's Book Club as this one was - guaranteeing bestseller status - I find myself wondering why or how Oprah, an entertainer, has been catapulted into an \\"authority\\" on what makes for good literature. While I enjoyed Strayed's descriptions of some of America's most wildly beautiful countryside of mountains and desert, I felt at times manipulated, suspecting the author knew she was going to turn this hike into a book, whether novel or memoir. Strayed's love for her mother, Bobbi, shines through, especially in one riveting scene where she is forced to shoot her mother's beloved horse, Lady. That scene alone elevated the book into the realm of literature . .. the horror of death,the betrayal the dying feel, while the living helplessly watch beauty crumble to its knees. This more than any words about stumbling in too-tight boots along the PCT spoke to me, felt most compelling and haunting. I could have done with one less bear, strange man or Snapple lemonade along the way.wildly beautiful countryside of mountains and desert, I felt at times manipulated, suspecting the author knew she was going to turn this hike into a book, whether novel or memoir. Shai has been trained to forge a replica of the original item to leave in its place. Her replicas are masterful. in many ways her replicas are better than the originals. The power of her work as she remakes her surroundings she amazes him, and every day he is more confused by her. This is not a love story--Gaotona struggles to understand why an artist of her caliber, who loves the craft as much as she does prostitutes her gift by making forgeries.<br /><br />Two factions now control her fate. They have something she needs, and she has something they need, but for how long? The Emperor Ashravan's condition has opened up new possibilities for some on the council, and they are ruthless. 1583334386|5.0|Many of us feel that we don't accomplish certain things because we generally lack willpower or lack it in those crucial moments when something needs to be done or avoided.  In this enlightening and entertaining book, psychology instructor Kelly McGonigal explains all the nuances of willpower, including it's breaking down into both \\"want\\" and \\"won't\\" power.  This is the book version of her hugely popular course at Stanford University.  McGonigal explains some of the counterintuitive ways that willpower works or is affected.  For example, showing graphic images of smoking-induced cancer may actually increase smoking.  Why?  Because those images cause stress in the smoker and what do smokers due to calm down?  Smoke!  Also, doing something good gives a person license to then do something bad.  Just gave a homeless person some change?  Your next move may be to use office supplies for personal use.  I am only giving a surface treatment here of the points made, obviously, but the book is rich in detail explaining why these behavioral phenomena exist.  Interestingly to me, some of McGonigal's claims seem to run counter to some claims from behavioral economics.  For example, the latter claims that placing healthy food items in a prominent location makes people eat more healthy and less junk food.  McGonigal shows that in some cases simply placing health food on a fast food menu increases the sale of unhealthy food.  Why?  Because people give themselves a sort of healthy thinking \\"credit\\" and thus license to misbehave.  I'd like to see how these viewpoints get reconciled.<br /><br />I had a very interesting experience myself while reading the book.  I totally pigged out on sweets, soda and other junk.  Why?  Because I figured, with all the willpower knowledge I was gaining, I would more than make up for it after I finished reading the book.  I may have also been motivated by all the stories of willpower failures.  Nevertheless, I do think that this book will go far in helping many people achieve their goals.  They just have to finish the book quickly.<br /><br />One other bonus with this book:  the author has a great sense of humor and her jokes throughout the book are quite funny.  Whatever.

1250012570|5.0|*Review posted on Mundie Moms on 5/31/13*<br /><br />Every year since I began reviewing, there seems to be a story that surprises me. It's usually a quietly launched story without a lot of over-hyped buzz, but it picks up steam as it passes from reviewer to reviewer and friend to friend. It's the story that your good, bookworm friends will text you about and insist you need to read it next. It's the story that once you do read, you understand immediately why your friends encouraged you to read it and so in turn, you pass it along to someone who hasn't read it, yet. Eleanor and Park is just such a story.<br /><br />First, I remember 1986 a little too well, and I remember the music a little too well. I also remember the biases of small towns towards anyone who didn't fit the norm. I use this phrase entirely too much, so here it goes again -- lovely, layered characters. That's exactly what Rainbow brings us. It's not just Eleanor and Park who I feel I know so well that when I climb onto a school bus (for my kids' field trips), I expect to see them in the back. It's also the parents, especially Park's, that rang so true. His mom could be my mom if you swap out her Korean upbringing for a Serbian one. Rainbow delivers glimpses of their back-stories in a few short sentences that left me shattered at what the mom, in particular, must have experienced. I won't even discuss Park's dad, because in Rainbow's capable hands he's THAT guy; oh trust me, you'll know it when you read it. He's the guy that is so consistent; I simply fell in love with both his parents. And in them, I saw hope for what Eleanor and Park could become.<br /><br />But wait, you say, Sophie this book is not called Park's parents. Okay, it's not. I'm trying to not spoil things because Eleanor lives a tough life. An awful life. In the midst of what should be hopeless despair, she finds pockets of sanity for herself, and she finds Park. Watching her allow Park into her life made this story, for me, one of the most satisfying reads this year. I won't even talk about the ending because it contained words that broke my heart, yes, in a good way.most satisfying reads this year. I won't even talk about the ending because it contained words that broke my heart, yes, in a good way.

B008AUGEFC|4.0|David Mitchell's 2004 Cloud Atlas is a novel in six stories: the journal of American traveler in the Pacific in 1850, the letters of a young Englishman working for a elderly composer in Belgium in 1931, a mystery story about an female investigative reporter in California in 1975, a film treatment about an aging English publisher in the present day, a Q and A with a cloned servant in dystopian Korean in 2144, and the experiences of a goatherd in post-apocalypse Hawaii in 2321. I found the book to be both less and more than I'd expected after hearing about it when the film version came out last year.<br /><br />Many readers alluded to the difficulty of the puzzling narrative structure, which I found to be far less of a problem than described. Mitchell's structure is actually pretty straightforward -- the stories unfold in chronological order but each is interrupted at a cliffhanger moment, with only the Hawaii portion told from beginning to end at roughly the midpoint in the novel. Then the other stories resume and are completed in reverse-chronological order, ending where the novel began with the Pacific journal. Although readers might lose track of elements of the interrupted stories, the narrative is easy to follow. Another element highlighted by many readers that underwhelmed me was the theme of reincarnation, especially the motif of an unusual birthmark that traced the movement of a single soul through time. The birthmark seemed gimmicky to me, especially since Mitchell didn't develop the idea beyond noting the presence of the birthmark on various characters.<br /><br />More important were the ways in which the book offered more than I'd expected. In each of these very different stories, Mitchell evoked a vivid sense of place, wove a variety of conflicts into an engaging story, and created a memorable voice. I was interested in each story individually and intrigued by they way those stories combined into the larger novel. He is clearly a very talented writer who rose to the difficult challenge he'd posed for himself in creating this complex novel. In addition, I was intrigued by the his deft use of archetypes in these tales: the captive redeemed, the sinner on the run, the corruption of power, the endurance of compassion. Several of those archetypical motifs -- and more -- could be found in each of his stories, and one of the pleasures of this novel for me was discerning the recurrence of those arcehtypes as I was reading and since I finished the novel.<br /><br />Finally, reading this book clarified for me something that I'd been mulling over for the past few months: Why do I read five or six books by novelists from Ireland or the UK for each novel I read by an American writer? American fiction is as polarized as American politics, with commercial fiction dominated by writers hoping to create a brand by devising a signature formula from a set of rigid expectations for each genre that they can repeat again and again, and literary fiction dominated by writers who pride themselves on creating unengaging characters to whom nothing much happens. Things seem to be very different across the pond where commercial writers, such as Ireland's Tana French and England's Nick Hornby, may work in identifiable genres but do not write endless iterations of the same formula. And many of the literary writers from across the sea, such as Ireland's Colm Toibin and England's Ian McEwan, deliver novels that can be enjoyed by those who read for entertainment and those who read for erudition. In Cloud Atlas, David Mitchell has married both strands of fiction -- genre and literary -- in a single novel that is both entertaining and erudite.rose to the difficult challenge he'd posed for himself in creating this complex novel. In addition, I was intrigued by the his deft use of archetypes in these tales: the captive redeemed, the sinner on the run, the corruption of power, the endurance of compassion. Several of those archetypical motifs -- and more -- could be found in each of his stories, and one of the pleasures of this novel for me was discerning the recurrence of those arcehtypes as I was reading and since I finished the novel.<br /><br />Finally, reading this book clarified for me something that I'd been mulling over for the past few months: Why do I read five or six books by novelists from Ireland or the UK for each novel I read by an American writer? American fiction is as polarized as American politics, with commercial fiction dominated by writers hoping to create a brand by devising a signature formula from a set of rigid expectations for each genre that they can repeat again and again, and literary fiction dominated by writers who pride themselves on creating unengaging characters to whom nothing much happens. Things seem to be very different across the pond where commercial writers, such as Ireland's Tana French and England's Nick Hornby, may work in identifiable genres but do not write endless iterations of the same formula. And many of the literary writers from across the sea, such as Ireland's Colm Toibin and England's Ian McEwan, deliver novels that can be enjoyed by those who read for entertainment and those who read for erudition. In Cloud Atlas, David Mitchell has married both strands of fiction -- genre and literary -- in a single novel that is both entertaining and erudite.

B00821OW8K|4.0|The first season of the post apocalyptic alien invasion adventure Falling Skies (2011), is generally entertaining, and has some exciting moments, but those looking for something more than a PG-13 adventure, are in for a bit of a disappointment.  Falling Skies doesn't feature graphic violence or brutality like FOX's 24.  Executive produced by Steven Spielberg, and broadcast on TNT, this series prominently features minors, and tends to soften the violence, minimize casualties, and tailor the material to be suitable for teens.<br /><br />Much must be taken on faith, beginning with a mysterious invasion by powerful robotic aliens, nicknamed \\"skidders\\".  These creatures capture children, take over their bodies, and use them to carry out their aims.  Using kids this way, just doesn't seem like the most efficient thing to do, but keeping blinders on your critical eye, often seems necessary, as the societal construct, and the way most characters behave, often seems open to question.<br /><br />The story centers on a group of survivors located near Boston, called the 2nd Massachusetts, a combination of 200 military personnel (\\"fighters\\"), and 100 civilians, led by Captain Weaver (Will Patton), who initially seems to resent the civilians.  Tom Mason (Noah Wyle), a former college professor, is second in command, and tasked with overseeing the welfare of the civilians.<br /><br />Focused on securing the basecamp, Weaver and his command, spend the first part of the season doing little fighting.  Instead it is Mason, whose son has been captured by the aliens, and an assortment of soldiers, civilians, and children, who mostly engage the enemy, usually as part of an effort to gather supplies, or rescue lost children.<br /><br />Cable TV has come a long way, but Falling Skies doesn't feature the same level of violence as The Walking Dead.  The numerous fights against CGI aliens, features tons of automatic fire, but with lots of flash and few casualties, the action scenes soon become mostly eye candy.  Interestingly,  most fatalities occur when humans kill each other.<br /><br />Discipline is generally very lax, as following orders often seems \\"optional\\".  Only when Weaver becomes delusional, does the matter of command really become an issue, for this less than cohesive group.  Their inability to communicate and gather information is a real handicap, and it takes up to Episode 9, for them to finally attempt to attack the enemy.  This effort soon fizzles out, as the season ends on a most intriguing note.<br /><br />Although just a handful of characters are fleshed out, the acting performances are pretty good.  Mason is cast in the superhero role, a fighter, a father, a leader, and perhaps a savior as well.  It's a little too much at times, but Noah Wyle, pulls it off fairly well.  Weaver isn't the ideal leader, but Will Patton brings some gruff humanity to the role.  Colin Cunningham as the renegade Pope, generates internal conflict, and provides some dark humor.  Moon Bloodgood is enjoyable, as the cheerful doctor for the unit.<br /><br />Season One contains only ten episodes, and to the show's credit, the plot generally does move forward during each one, revealing more about a situation that is mostly shrouded in mystery.  What unfolds is a scenario with some cool new elements, as well as some very familiar post apocalyptic themes.  With teens as front line fighters, and PG-13 sensibilities strongly emphasized, Falling Skies aims for a wide audience.  The potential is there for something special, but the writing falters in too many places, as bullets and battles are too often used as a solution.  Falling Skies is entertaining, but its shortcomings make it a frustrating watch at times.  Rating: 3.5 stars.mostly eye candy. B009J2VMXY|5.0|[hint: for ease of navigation, read the review though to the end, then come back and click on the links.]<br /><br />The Universal Box contains 38 recordings of the Rite of Spring on 19 CDs.<br />The rival Sony Box has 10 performances on 10 CDs: [[ASIN:B00AYQHPDK Igor Stravinsky: Le Sacre Du Printemps 100th Anniv]]<br />EMI has yet to be heard from.<br />This is getting out of hand.<br /><br />All this set me to thinking: What are the truly essential recordings of Stravinsky's Rite of Spring?<br />Not the BEST PERFORMANCE or the BEST RECORDING, but the MOST IMPORTANT recordings of the music since its debut in 1913.<br />Recordings every serious Stravinskian should own.<br /><br />I came up with a list of thirteen recordings.<br />- Stravinsky was notoriously superstitious; I don't think he would like this.<br /><br />The earliest was recorded in 1921, the most recent in 1969.<br />Sony includes five of them in their box (50%).<br />Universal includes just three, out of of the 38 in their box (Ouch).<br /><br />Three are conducted by the composer.<br />Three are conducted by Pierre Monteux, the conductor at the first performance in 1913.<br />[Monteux began his conducting career in 1895 and was active well into the 1960s].<br /><br />1) 1921 - Igor Stravinsky played The Rite on a pianola which created a piano roll: [[ASIN:B001RILB20 Stravinsky: The Rite of Spring]] - or - [[ASIN:B000000TPX Stravinsky: The Rite of Spring]]<br />Later on he was able to speed up certain sections, or add additional notes by punching new holes in the piano roll.<br />Apparently he did this a lot. He was not a virtuoso pianist.<br />The exact tempo is always controversial with piano rolls, but they do preserve the tempo relationships the composer wanted (seven years after the premiere).<br /><br />2) 1929 - Pierre Monteux, Orchestre Symphonique de Paris: [[ASIN:B000027QGN Stravinsky: Rite of Spring and Petrushka]] - or - [[ASIN:B00000G2IR Orchestre Symphonique de Paris, Vol. 2.]]<br />3) 1929 - Igor Stravinsky, Walther Straram Concerts Orchestra: [[ASIN:B00008FITD Rite of Spring / Firebird]]<br />Sixteen years after the premiere, EMI recorded competing versions by the composer, and by the conductor of that first performance.<br />These two are as close as we can get to what the first night audience heard (minus the yelling).<br />Both are in primitive sound with barely competent French orchestras.<br />The orchestra at the first performance wasn't that great either.<br />The Monteux is a mess, the Stravinsky even messier. Both are essential.<br />I wish EMI or Naxos would couple them on a single CD with informed program notes.<br /><br />4) 1930 - Leopold Stokowski, Philadelphia Orchestra - in the Sony Box or ASIN: B00000E6PA *<br />Stokowski conducted the American premiere of the work in 1920.<br />His was the best-selling Rite on 78s.<br />The Rite of Spring became a fixture in popular culture after Stokowski conducted it in Walt Disney's &#34;Fantasia&#34;.<br />You can also hear Part Two as suspense music (uncredited) in lots of low-budget Hollywood films from the '30s through the '50s).<br /><br />5) 1940 - Igor Stravinsky, New York Philharmonic - in the Sony Box or ASIN: B00AL6SN52 *<br />The composer's second go at the work, with a much better orchestra than 1929 (plus his conducting skills had improved).<br /><br />6) 1951 - Pierre Monteux, Boston Symphony - in the Sony Box or ASIN: B000003FLV or ASIN: B00000E6C7 *<br />Monteux's second recording, with one of the great orchestras. Mono sound is the only drawback.<br /><br />7) 1956 - Pierre Monteux, Paris Conservatoire Orchestra - in the Universal Box or ASIN: B000G6BJKS *<br />Monteux's third recording, the first stereo recording of the work.<br />The orchestra is less good than the Boston Symphony, but it does have the quintessential French orchestral sound (now lost).<br /><br />8) 1957 - Ernest Ansermet, L'Orchestre de la Suisse Romande - in the Universal Box or ASIN: B00005ND3M or ASIN:B000004242 *<br />On Stravinsky's recommendation, Ansermet was appointed Monteux's successor as music director of the Ballets Russe.<br />He conducted the first revival of the Rite of Spring in 1916, and was close to the composer for the next twenty years.<br /><br />9) 1958 - Leonard Bernstein, New York Philharmonic: [[ASIN:B00BQSSGD8 Stravinsky: Le Sacre Du Printemps]]<br />This was the best-selling Rite of Spring in the early stereo era.<br />People bought it to show off their new Hi-Fi Stereo equipment.<br />It popularized the work on LP much like Stokowski had done on 78s.<br />For some reason, Sony left it out of their box, opting instead for Bernstein's less interesting 1972 remake with the London Symphony<br />(Universal has his even less interesting 1982 remake with the Israel Philharmonic).<br /><br />10) 1960 - Igor Stravinsky, Columbia Symphony - in the Sony Box or ASIN: B00AL6SN52 *<br />The composer's third attempt, the only one in stereo. His conducting skills kept getting better.<br />This was the New York version of the &#34;Columbia Symphony&#34; - basically the New York Philharmonic.<br /><br />11) 1964 - Herbert von Karajan, Berlin Philharmonic - in the Universal Box or ASIN: B000W99IJI *<br />Included in this list as an example of what not to do.<br />Stravinsky famously trashed this recording in Hi-Fi Stereo Review (February, 1965):<br />&#34;..the performance [is] too polished, a pet savage rather than a real one...I doubt whether The Rite can be satisfactory performed in terms of Herr von Karajan's traditions. I do not mean to imply that he is out of his depths, however, but rather that he is in my shallows...There are simply no regions for soul-searching in The Rite of Spring.&#34;<br />[Perhaps chastened by this review, Karajan's 1977 remake is both more conventional and less interesting]<br /><br />12) 1966 - Yevgeny Svetlanov, USSR Symphony: Stravinsky: [[ASIN:B000H49NFM Stravinsky: Rite of Spring / Petrushka]] - or - [[ASIN:B00176OA2K Igor Stravinsky- The rite of spring, Card game]] - or - [[ASIN:B0000E6XK3 Stravinsky-Rite of Spring/Jeu de Cartes]]<br />My wild card choice.<br />In the same article where he trashed Karajan, Stravinsky had high praise for a 1962 concert by this same USSR Symphony, conducted by Robert Craft:<br />&#34;...whereas the French orchestra turned the Rite into a French piece, and the German orchestra into a German piece, the Russian turns into a Russian piece which is just right...compare this to America where it has become a conductor's showpiece for one-rehearsal concerts in the Hollywood Bowl!&#34;<br />Stravinsky wasn't praising Craft's conducting.<br />He was praising the Russian sound of the orchestra, which is exactly what he wanted.<br />Since the fall of Communism, Russian orchestras have lost this unique sound.<br />This is not the case with the oldest Russian recording I could find: Svetlanov's 1966 USSR Symphony has a sound that is lost to history, but it is precisely this sound that Stravinsky had in mind when composing the Rite of Spring.<br /><br />13) 1969 - Pierre Boulez, Cleveland Orchestra - in the Sony Box or ASIN: B000002A2M *<br />In a sense, this was the first modern recording of The Rite of Spring.<br />It created a sensation when first released;<br />No one had ever heard an orchestra play this music with such precision.<br />Something that later recordings take for granted.<br />As to the performance itself, in 1970 Stravinsky gave it a qualified recommendation (he preferred his own - No surprise.)<br />Boulez's 1992 Cleveland remake - in the Universal Box - is just one of the many note-perfect recordings that came later.<br /><br />If you're counting: Numbers 1, 2, 3, 9, 12 are not part of any box He was praising the Russian sound of the orchestra, which is exactly what he wanted. Since the fall of Communism, Russian orchestras have lost this unique sound. This is not the case with the oldest Russian recording I could find: Svetlanov's 1966 USSR Symphony has a sound that is lost to history, but it is precisely this sound that Stravinsky had in mind when composing the Rite of Spring.  13) 1969 - Pierre Boulez, Cleveland Orchestra - in the Sony Box or ASIN: B000002A2M * In a sense, this was the first modern recording of The Rite of Spring. It created a sensation when first released; No one had ever heard an orchestra play this music with such precision. Something that later recordings take for granted. B008JFUUIA|4.0|What do you get if you mash together Bring It On and Coyote Ugly? You get something that's oh-so Pitch Perfect. The Barden Bellas are Barden's first-ever, all-girl a cappella singing group that rely on their looks and antiquated pop songs to get by in competition. After a disasterous incident at the previous year's finals, the Bellas are back and looking to redeem themselves. Enter Beca (Anna Kendrick). Beca's is a girl who's ambitions lie more with DJ'ing than in her studies and lucky for the Bellas, Beca can sing. And if she wants to get ahead in school, she'll have to do it a cappella style. Soon she's breathing new life, a different look and a fresh sound in to the struggling girl group. Add a pinch of friendly rivalry with Barden's all-male a cappella group, The TrebleMakers, a dash of flirtation and a bit of raunchy humour and you're ready to be pitch slapped outta this world!<br /><br />Okay, I wasn't exactly chomping at the bit to see Pitch Perfect mostly based on the fact that I can't stand the show, Glee, but I got bored one night with my Netflix queue and decided to give it a whirl. Let me just tell you something right now ---- I am soooooo happy I did because this movie was AMAZING! Seriously. I don't know what it is about these types of movies but they put me on this weird musical high. Yeah. It's freaking crazy!<br /><br />But you know what's even crazier? Just how much I liked the movie despite all the wrongness with it. I mean, there was a lot that didn't really work for me but the fact that the good parts were SO good, it pretty much outweighed all of the bad. Ah, the power of musicals. They can do virtually no wrong in my eyes. And I know I'm kinda categorising Pitch Perfect as a musical, but it isn't so in the traditional sense of the term. The majority of the movie isn't spelled out solely via the song and dance numbers, but the heart of the film does lie in its songs. If you remove everything but the musical numbers, you'd still get the overall essense of the movie sans the raunch and quick-wit. So basically, the whole story is told through the music and I'm very much okay with that.<br /><br />I guess my biggest issue with the film was that the plot was sometimes a little too contrived and some of the chemistry felt forced. For instance, there was an unnecessary love triangle which I totally wasn't buying. In fact, I was even sure it was supposed to be a love triangle. They could've just removed that character and/or bit altogether. Also, much of the raunchier humour (not that there was a lot of it) could've been completely omitted because it just wasn't needed. The movie was funny enough on its own. It was like they were trying to be Bridesmaids only they didn't quite get there.<br /><br />Be that as it may, I will say that the humour of the film was much more elevated than that of most teen comedies today. The comedy lies in the sharp-tongued characters, the witty dialogue and the fact that it wasn't afraid to poke a bit of fun at a cappella while still managing to make it seem super cool. Pitch Perfect verges on the border of satirical without going all the way there, making it more appealing to the masses. Round it out with amazing ad-libbing from Rebel Wilson (love this girl!), Anna Kendrick, Brittany Snow, Anna Camp and Elizabeth Banks and there's your girl power. I immediately downloaded the soundtrack after I watched it which has been on replay all week long, I might add! There really isn't much more I can say other than watch it because I think, like me, you'll be pleasantly surprised!<br /><br />Food for Thought: Pitch Perfect is a modern musical that takes a cappella music to the next level, giving it a fresh, modern and pitch perfect feel! 0307477479|5.0|Most chapters of this novel work incredibly well as self contained short stories. Some connect together in ways both large and tenuous and give you an \\"a-ha!\\" moment. Some could almost border post-modern formal exercise territory. So, I can see why some readers are somewhat unsure what to make of this novel - but all of that being said, I think Egan has done a masterful job of giving you just enough pieces of the puzzle to be able to weave a coherent, straight ahead narrative, if that's your bent. On the other hand, the themes and times she tackles with sure handed technique that would fizzle in lesser hands are anything but coherently straight forward, and I think that's part of the point of the formal choices she deftly executes. Growing up, growing old, selling out, art vs commerce, living by ones ideals vs putting food on the table, overcoming the past, surviving the future: these are some of the motifs that weave their way through these richly told chapters. Whenever a writer steps out onto the cutting edge in terms of formal playfulness/innovation, to me the greatest pitfall they always risk is sacrificing the human heart and soul in their characters. Yet, what grabbed me almost immediately and riveted me chapter after chapter is how warmly and sympathetically the author treats the souls that populate her world. I constantly found myself in total empathy with a vast array of people, nodding my head, thinking \\"yes, this nails what it is to be alive\\" and this shining, powerful gift is Egan's greatest achievement. My life is richer for having read it - you can't ask for more than that from the arts. I would recommend this to anyone and everyone who is after an intelligent read by an author at the top of her game.

B000MCIBB6|5.0|I have owned and listened to this cd for forty years, and I have to say that for its time and milieu it is nothing short of daring and brilliant.  I often wonder how Morrison and crew could have conceived of such an arrangement of haunting, mood-inducing, primitive and archetypal sounds--so lovely and provoking. You only have to imagine a rainy, melancholy day (Rimbaud? Beaudelaire?) and staring out of a window as rain drops slide down the pane--the ultimate Romantic sensibility.  Yes, the easy answer is drugs, and there is plenty of drug-induced emotions and sounds on this cd, but it touches something wonderfully primeval and free, a gypsy-like tour-de-force of rock indulgence and creative angst.  I consider this cd to be one of the most creative and innovative recordings of its time, and if you will only listen to Morrison's incredible baritone and the instrumentals by the rest of the band (Ray M's classically trained rifts)you can only regard this as one of the finest and most imaginative recordings (yes, moody and dark, but wonderfully so) for its time. The \\"UnKnown Soldier\\" is one of the finest anti-war songs I've ever heard and \\"Love Street\\" is a knock-out song that is always psychically rejuvenating.  \\"Hello, I Love You,\\" the pop hit on this cd--although a decent song--is the weakest. Still, this is a pivotol cd in rock history.

The love story was very mild; the competitors fall in love through their shared experience of being involuntarily bound to the contest, and their admiration of each other's work.<br /><br />I was hoping the ending would make sense of the rest of the story, but the only thing I got from it was an explanation as to why the two (immortal???) magicians were at odds with each other in the first place, and a poetic discourse on the art of storytelling.<br /><br />Other bits and pieces:<br />Way, way too descriptive.  Gargantuan paragraphs are used to describe details that do nothing to move the story forward.  This tired me out and I had to take breaks (meaning days and sometimes, weeks) at several points throughout the book before feeling compelled to continue reading.<br />Overuse of \\"proper\\" and \\"save for\\".<br />Non-linear storytelling which I found unnecessary, although some readers may find it clever.<br />A secondary storyline whose main character at times comes across as the entire novel's main character, causing some confusion as to whose story the novel is really about.<br />Victorian-era customs and protocol (at least those expected of \\"proper\\" folk) were completely ignored; characters behave more like modern-day individuals.<br />Vague characters meant to come across as mysterious, enigmatic, etc., only came across as under-developed.  The one (slight) exception being Mr. A. H./\\"the man in the grey suit\\" - one of the magicians.<br />The circus Clock defies logic, even when suspending belief for the sake of the story.<br />The story takes place in several locations in the U.S. and Europe, but you wouldn't know it if it weren't spelled out for you, as there are little to no descriptions (go figure) to distinguish these locations from each other.<br /><br />Moments I enjoyed:<br />The scene where the heroine auditions for the circus, especially the finale.<br />The Pool of Tears and a few other circus attractions.<br />The hero's reaction when he realizes who his \\"competitor\\" is.<br />The chemistry between the hero and his first love.<br />The moment the drunk, eccentric proprietor comes across information about the magic being used.<br /><br />Overall, I would recommend this novel if plot is not an issue to the reader.  There are some creative elements regarding the circus itself that are worth experiencing.  The movie version of this book will need to be heavily re-written, however. I had never smiled so genuinely at the slightest expressions of affection or felt so deeply moved by the painful circumstances occurring in the lives of literary characters.  I don't think I am stretching too far to say that I felt emotions that I never knew I possessed. Hosseini was real in a way that I had not encountered \\"real\\" before. It is not my intention to delve into the many plot lines.  You will read them.  And you will read the many moving themes and the familiar and tragic qualities of human relationships.  And you will encounter Hosseini's wonderfully crafted imagery, his attention to detailand subtle nuance, his expertly realized characters and his complex and inspired dialogue. I think that Hosseini is saying--not only about the characters in the book but about all people--essentially this: each person has an interesting story to tell, and no life is unimportant.<br /><br />As I journeyed through the novel, I found myself compelled and inspired to change. I kept getting the tracks stuck in my head and thinking, \\"This can't be a Hooper track, can it?\\" because I had previously found his soundtracks so bland and forgettable.  I pulled out the soundtrack again today and, lo and behold, I now enjoy a substantial amount of it... albeit because of the game, not the movie.<br /><br />I think Hooper's style is just too subtle and minimalistic for the big screen.  I've actually always been a fan of \\"Professor Umbridge,\\" which is one of the only tracks that lives up to its potential.  It's not fun to listen to, per se, but it's not supposed to be.  It's appropriately cutesy on the surface, but rather grating and irritating as well, as Umbridge should be.  I think it's one of the better written character themes in the series.  It's memorably irritating.<br /><br />Tracks I grew to enjoy include \\"Another Story,\\" \\"Dumbledore's Army,\\" and \\"The Room of Requirement.\\"  Maybe they were just the ones played the most in LEGO Harry Potter...  But I don't think that's the whole story.  \\"Another Story\\" makes for a very atmospheric opener (to the film, that is; the soundtrack is out of order), and \\"The Room of Requirement\\" is appropriately whimsical.  It's actually used for the montage in the film which features the students in the Room of Requirement contrasted with humorous scenes of Filch trying to figure out what they're up to, and I think it works well for that.  Hooper does whimsy and humor well, but his score never reaches the dramatic crescendos it should have.  Therein lies the problem.<br /><br />In the end, I can now see Hooper's talent, but he's mismatched with this series.  The music is too minimalistic for a film of this heft.  I'm glad they brought in Alexandre Desplat for the final films, though this score has its appeal if you listen to it enough (or play LEGO Harry Potter for hours and hours...).
